    Discovery of 4,6- and 5,7-Disubstituted Isoquinoline Derivatives as a Novel Class of Protein Kinase C ζ Inhibitors with Fragment-Merging Strategy

    Abstract

    Two chemical series of novel protein kinase C ζ (PKCζ) inhibitors, 4,6-disubstituted and 5,7-disubstituted isoquinolines, were rapidly identified using our fragment merging strategy. This methodology involves biochemical screening of a high concentration of a monosubstituted isoquinoline fragment library, then merging hit isoquinoline fragments into a single compound. Our strategy can be applied to the discovery of other challenging kinase inhibitors without protein–ligand structural information. Furthermore, our optimization effort identified the highly potent and orally available 5,7-isoquinoline 37 from the second chemical series. Compound 37 showed good efficacy in a mouse collagen-induced arthritis model. The in vivo studies suggest that PKCζ inhibition is a novel target for rheumatoid arthritis (RA) and that 5,7-disubstituted isoquinoline 37 has the potential to elucidate the biological consequences of PKCζ inhibition, specifically in terms of therapeutic intervention for RA.

    Introduction

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To
    Protein kinase C ζ (PKCζ) is a member of the PKC family of kinases, which comprises 11 members grouped into three categories based on their molecular structure and mode of activation. The 11 PKC isoforms can be subdivided into diacylglycerol- (DAG) and calcium-dependent classical isoforms (PKCs α, βI, βII, and γ), DAG-dependent novel isoforms (PKCs δ, ε, η, and θ), and DAG- and calcium-independent atypical isoforms (PKCs λ, ι, and ζ).(1,2)
    PKCζ is involved in multiple signal transduction pathways, for example, as an intermediary in the activation of the NF-κB(3) and IL-4/Stat6 pathways, and in the regulation of cell polarity.(4,5) Several studies have shown that PKCζ is an attractive target for treating numerous diseases, such as diabetes,(6,7) proliferative vitreoretinopathy,(8) chronic inflammatory skin defects,(9) and breast cancer.(10) Our interest is in orthopedics, and thus we were interested that PKCζ effectively regulates tumor necrosis factor α (TNFα)-induced inflammatory response in arthritis.(3) The role of PKCζ in autoimmune and chronic inflammatory disease involves coactivation of the transcriptional activity of NF-κB, generated by the phosphorylation by PKCζ of Ser311 on the RelA subunit of NF-κB.(11−14) Pfizer (formerly known as Wyeth) researchers concluded that PKCζ is an atypical PKC isoform that serves as a critical link to NF-κB activation by TNF and IL-1 in chondrocytes. Another key finding was that siRNA-gene silencing of PKCζ and ι inhibits the expression of collagenase, matrix metalloproteinase (MMP)-1 and MMP-13 and inhibits cartilage destruction, via STAT3 and ERK-dependent c-fos induction.(15) PKCζ thus represents a potentially important disease-associated gene responsible for arthritis cartilage destruction and is an attractive therapeutic target. Orally active small molecule inhibitors of PKCζ could provide targeted and powerful therapeutic options for the treatment of chronic arthritis by blocking a common signaling component for multiple inflammatory cytokines, as well as interrupting arthritis progression that lie downstream of NF-κB.(15) Currently, however, few small molecules have been disclosed exhibiting either moderate activity or low selectivity against kinases. Representative examples are shown in Figure 1.
    The natural product spheciosterol sulfate C (1) inhibits NF-κB activation by inhibiting PKCζ.(16) Pfizer researchers identified 2-(6-phenyl-1H-indazole-3-yl)-1H-benzodimidazole (2) as a PKCζ inhibitor that binds to the ATP binding pocket of the kinase.(17) Additionally, cancer research investigators identified thieno[2,3-d]pyrimidine (3) as an atypical PKC inhibitor,(18) and Matius et al. discovered 1,3,5-trisubstituted 4 as a potent and selective example of a class of allosteric PKCζ inhibitors.(19,20) All of these compounds most likely act on the 3-phosphoinositide-dependent protein kinase-interacting fragment (PIF)-pocket in the kinase catalytic domain, given their strongly reduced in vitro and cellular activities toward a PIF-pocket mutant of PKCζ. Several cell-based assays confirmed that PKCζ is also targeted in the cellular environment and provided evidence that these putative PIF-pocket-directed compounds affect phosphorylation of the activation loop in the catalytic domain. Although allosteric strategies have shown excellent selectivity against other kinases, their potencies remain low in enzymatic assays and cell studies. Our aim is to identify a small molecule with high potency in cell studies for target validation. To this end, we used the systemic merging strategy of our originated target-focused fragment library to identify a type 1 inhibitor with high potency in both kinase enzyme and cell assays.

    Results and Discussion

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To

    Identification of High Concentration Hit Fragments by Biochemical Screening

    Fragment-based drug discovery (FBDD) encompasses a broad range of methodologies and strategies for finding small fragment molecules with affinity against a target protein. Fragment molecules are typically low molecular weight and are selected to be more polar and soluble than larger compounds used for high-through-put screening (HTS).(21) The hit rate of fragment screening is typically higher than conventional HTS screening, and thus hit compounds tend to be less complex and exhibit structurally broader variation. These characteristic features can aid the interpretation of structure–activity relationship (SAR) data and accelerate their optimization.(21−23) Typically, the binding mode of a fragment is identified from the X-ray crystal structure of the fragment bound to its target protein, enabling a rational evolution of hit fragments. However, the crystal structure of PKCζ is currently unavailable, and thus an alternative method was required to identify binders to PKCζ. Our strategy is to biochemically screen PKCζ kinase with a high concentration of our kinase-focused fragment library, comprising approximately 5000 monosubstituted isoquinoline fragment compounds. The isoquinoline structure is a kinase hinge binder(24) potentially conducive to identifying selective type 1 kinase inhibitors. An example of the isoquinoline structure binding mode with kinase is shown in Figure 2. The nitrogen in isoquinoline (in this case, fasudil (5)) mediates hydrogen bonds to the kinase hinge region and the hydrophobic part of the isoquinoline ring occupies an ATP pocket, a typical binding mode. Each kinase ATP binding pocket has its own characteristics, in particular with respect to binding the hydrophobic part of the isoquinoline ring. Thus, we designed isoquinoline fragments monosubstituted on the 1-, 3-, 4-, 5-, 6-, 7-, or 8-positions of the isoquinoline ring. We anticipated that high concentration biochemical screening of monosubstituted isoquinoline fragments would provide many hit fragments. Furthermore, if we could identify two fragments substituted on different positions, they could be merged on the same isoquinoline ring, rapidly providing a highly potent molecule without the need for structural information. We should note that similar approach was previously performed by Plexxikon′s called as scaffold-based discovery.(25) In this approach, they built large libraries of target-focused libraries which were screened biochemically and successfully identified a selective inhibitor of oncogenic B-Raf kinase with potent antimelanoma activity (Vemurafenib).(26)
    But to return to our strategy, an additional advantage of using isoquinoline fragments is that the 1-, 3-, 4-, 5-, 6-, 7-, and 8-carbons on the isoquinoline ring can be substituted, potentially encompassing an enormous accessible chemical space.(27) As mentioned above, this characteristic also can be utilized to easily construct a monosubstituted isoquinoline library while retaining structural diversity rather than other heterocycles included two or three heteroatoms, such as pyrido[3,4-d]pyrimidine, pyrido[3,2-c]pyridazine, and so on. We constructed a fragment library of approximately 5000 monosubstituted isoquinolines and conducted biochemical screening of PKCζ kinase using a fragment library concentration of 100 μM.
    Analysis of all the assay results identified the hit isoquinoline fragments. The representatives are drawn in Figure 3. Moreover, all fragment structures are shown in Supporting Information, Tables S1 and S2. We looked for structural patterns in the hit fragments. Both fragments 7 and 8 have a saturated nitrogen-containing heterocycle, exhibit good potency, and have low molecular weight (Mw) (Mw of 7, 227.31; Mw of 8, 214.27). Comparing with the homopiperazine structure of fasudil (5), the structural features of 7 and 8 might result in similar interactions with acidic areas of PKCζ kinase. In contrast, fragments 6 and 9 have an aromatic ring and show lower PKCζ inhibitory activity but their aniline groups might interact with a specific amino acid residue in PKCζ. We thus designed a merged structure, with one saturated heterocycle and one aromatic ring on the isoquinoline ring, representative of merging fragments 6 and 8, or fragments 7 and 9. In practice, merging fragments 6 and 7 or fragments 8 and 9 could be synthetically challenging due to the close proximity of the two substituents and thus we designed and synthesized 4,6-disubstituted isoquinoline, the merged molecule of fragments 6 and 8.

    Optimization of 4,6-Disubstituted Isoquinoline

    4,6-Disubstituted isoquinoline 10 was synthesized, then evaluated by biochemical assays (Figure 4). The IC50 of 10 indicated a significant increase in potency (0.033 μM) compared to fragment 8 (a 424-fold increase), prompting us to further evolve the 4- and 6-disubstituted isoquinoline compound from starting molecule 10.
    We investigated the SAR of aryl groups on the 4-position by preparing a subset library of the 4,6-isoquinoline derivatives 11–21; the SAR results are shown in Table 1. The 4-amino-phenyl group substitution (10) showed good potency, whereas the 3-hydroxy (11) substitution exhibited less potency. We then replaced 4-Ar with other heterocycles and found that 2-thienyl (12), 3-thienyl (13), 3-furyl (14), 2-thiazolyl (15), and 2-pyridyl (16) exhibited decreased potency. Replacement of the Ar with bicyclic-heterocycles (17–20) did not improve potency compared to (7), whereas substitution with the 1H-pyrrolo[2,3-b]pyridin-5-yl group (21) achieved greatly improved potency, and thus we optimized the 6-position on the isoquinoline ring.
    Table 1. SAR of Ar Groups on the 4-Position of Isoquinoline
    no.ArPKCζ IC50 (μM)
    104-amino-phenyl0.033
    113-hydroxy-phenyl0.142
    122-thienyl0.147
    133-thienyl0.141
    143-furyl1.253
    152-thiazolyl3.638
    162-pyridyl>40
    173-quinolyl1.074
    182-naphthyl0.356
    192-benzoxazolyl1.014
    202-benzthiazolyl0.631
    211H-pyrrolo[2,3-b]pyridin-5-yl0.013
    Extension of the carbon linker (22) and placing the nitrogen at a different position (23) did not improve potency. The piperidine structure (24) showed good potency, but again, extension of the linker (25) was not promising. We concluded that (S)-pyrrolidinol (10) is the optimal 6-position substituent and thus we further profiled 21 (Table 2).
    Table 2. SAR of the R1 Group on the 6-Position of Isoquinoline
    The kinase selectivity of 21 was evaluated using a Carnabio Science profiling kit. The results suggested excellent selectivity against 216 kinases, each present at 100 nM (for detailed results, see Supporting Information, Table S3). In vivo mouse PK studies of 21 showed no significant detrimental biological effects (Table 3).
    Table 3. In Vivo Mouse PK Studies (2 mg/10 mL/kg, po 21)
    propertyvalues

    protein binding (%)83.1
    Finally, we conducted a cell assay for the production of TNFα using lipopolysaccharide (LPS)-stimulated THP-1 human acute monocytic leukemia cells and obtained an IC50 of 8.0 μM, suggesting the inhibition of TNFα production. Animal arthritis model studies require more potent compounds in cell assays, and thus we investigated an alternative chemical series from our hit fragments.

    Optimization of 5,7-Disubstituted Isoquinoline

    FBDD can enable multiple chemical series from hit fragments, allowing the use of the same or different hit compounds. We merged fragments 7 and 9 and designed 5,7-disubstituted isoquinolines in a manner similar to 4,6-disubstituted isoquinoline 10 (Figure 5). The inhibitory activity of 26 was significantly increased (IC50 = 0.5 nM) compared to 7. However, the selectivity of 26 against PKA is not satisfied (10 nM, 20-fold of PKA/PKCζ), and in vivo pharmacokinetic studies of 26 showed a low PK profile (T1/2, 3.4 h; Tmax, 2 h; Cmax, 30 nM; AUC, 122.18 nM·h at 2 mg/kg po) by the highly polar structure. Our hypothesis was that the alternative structure of aniline or the increase of the lipophilicity of 26 could improve the total PK profile to avoid rapid metabolism. Our optimization efforts of 26 focused next on achieving a balance between acceptable kinase selectivity and PK profile by seeking an alternative structure of aniline or increasing hydrophobicity.
    The aryl group on the 7-substituent of the isoquinoline ring was first optimized and studied by SAR (Table 4). To avoid an outer charged structure, anilines at the meta or ortho positions on the phenyl group (27 and 28) were designed. However, both anilines led to decreased activity and worse selectivity against PKD2. Because those results were obtained. PKD2 selectivity of the synthesized compounds has been monitored by evaluating PKCζ activity. Phenol at the para position (29) showed good potency, but its selectivity against PKD2 was worse than 3. An ortho phenol group (30) showed decreased potency and PKD2 selectivity, and a phenyl group (31) and acetamide (32) decreased potency as well. Therefore, the 4-aniline structure exhibited the best potency and selectivity against PKD2 in this SAR study. Next, we optimized the substituent on the 5-position with respect to both potency and PKD2 selectivity.
    Table 4. SAR of the R2 Group on the 7-Position of Isoquinoline
    no.R2PKCζ IC50 (μM)PKD2 IC50 (μM)PKD2/PKCζ (fold)
    264-NH2-phenyl-0.00050.246492
    273-NH2-phenyl-0.1620.1020.63
    282-NH2-phenyl-0.1530.1871.22
    294-HO-phenyl-0.00060.01728
    302-HO-phenyl-0.0540.2634.87
    31phenyl0.00640.26141
    324-acetyl-NH-phenyl-0.0130.76259
    A summary of the SAR results on the 5-position optimization studies is shown in Table 5. We identified the stereochemistry of eutomer (S)-isomer 33, which showed higher potency than (R)-isomer 34. Next, we investigated the decomposed ring structure of amine 35 and confirmed the inevitability of the rigid structure for retaining its potency. The potency of the 7-membered ring compound (36) retained the potency of 3 but exhibited improved PKD2 selectivity and PK profile (T1/2, 1.1 h; Tmax, 0.25 h; Cmax, 134 nM; AUC, 384.6 nM·h at 2 mg/kg po) by expanding the ring size to improve polarity compared to 3, indicating that an azepane ring is optimal on the 5-position of isoquinoline. However, there was still the issue of the selectivity of PKA on 36 (10.4-fold of PKCζ). Thus, the further optimization of 36 was required.
    Table 5. SAR of the R2 Group on the 5-Position of Isoquinoline
    As mentioned above, the azepane moiety is the important structure for the selectivity of PKD2 and PK profile. Therefore, the 7-aryl group was optimized while retaining the 5-azepane-isoquinoline structure, and the results are shown in Table 6. Introduction of a fluorine atom on the phenyl group next to isoquinoline (37) improved selectivity against PKA and PKCζ activity was unchanged. Difluorine substituent scanning showed that 2,3-difluorine (38) and 2,6-difluorine (39) had potencies similar to 37 but decreased selectivities against other kinases compared to monofluorine substituents. The phenyl group was revisited and 40 showed moderate activity, but introduction of the 2,6-difluoro substituent (41) was not effective. The selectivity of 2-aminopyridine (42) against PKA was improved, and potency toward PKCζ was maintained. 2-Amino-6-fluoropyridine (43) and 2-amino-4-fluoropyridine (44) showed only moderate PKCζ inhibitory activity but excellent selectivity against PKA. In comparison with 37, 44 does not satisfy the requirements for PKCζ inhibitory activity and thus we further profiled 37.
    Table 6. Final Optimization Results
    Next, we used 37 in THP-1/LPS TNFα cell assays (HTRF) and obtained an IC50 of 0.045 μM for the inhibition of TNFα production. The kinase selectivity of 37 was evaluated in duplicate using a ProQuinase profiling kit at one concentration against 368 protein kinases and 13 lipid kinases. The results showed no other significant inhibition, except for 50% inhibition of proto-oncogene tyrosine-protein kinase (FYN) and 49% inhibition of tropomyosin receptor kinase B (TrkB) (for detailed results, see Supporting Information, Table S4). In vivo mouse PK studies of 37 showed good PK profile and no significant detrimental biological effects (Table 7). In retrospect, we suspect that the reason for this is an improvement in the PK profile. We thus chose 37 as a candidate as an in vivo mouse collagen-induced arthritis (CIA) model compound.
    Table 7. In Vivo Mouse PK Studies (2 mg/10 mL/kg po and 1 mg/10 mL/kg, iv 37)
    propertyvalues
    T1/2 (h)1.74
    Tmax (h)0.25
    Cmax (nM)261
    AUC last (nM·h)395.2
    Vd (L/kg)6.63
    CL (L/h/kg)3.12
    protein binding (%)92.5
    We evaluated the anti-inflammatory effect of 37 by administering it to CIA mice, using BMS 345541,(28) an inhibitor of nuclear factor kappa-B kinase subunit (IKK) α, and β inhibitor as positive controls in the CIA model. The administration of 3, 10, or 30 mg/kg of 37 dose-dependently ameliorated CIA progression, with reduced joint swelling in a dose-dependent manner, whereas the vehicle control mice showed severe swelling after booster immunization (Figures 6 and 7). Similar results were observed with 60 mg/kg of BMS 345541, suggesting that PKCζ inhibition is a novel therapeutic target of RA and that 5,7-disubstituted isoquinoline 37 has the potential to elucidate the biological consequences of PKCζ inhibition, specifically in terms of therapeutic intervention for RA. Additionally, mode of action was confirmed with the phosphorylation of p65Ser311 and IκB with Western blot. The p65 is a key factor for the phosphorylation of RelA because of the possible to form the complex of RelA/p65 and IκB is an upstream of PKCζ signal cascade.(11−14,29) The results of Western blot showed compound 37 and BMS 345541 inhibited the phosphorylation on this site, and it agrees with in vivo results (for detailed results, see Supporting Information, Figures S1 and S2).

    Homology Model Studies of Compounds 10 and 26 with PKCζ

    Because the crystal structure of PKCζ is unknown, homology modeling was conducted to reveal the binding mode of the key compounds 10 and 26 with PKCζ. The crystal structure of PKCι (PDB 4DC2)(30) was used as a template to build 100 PKCζ homology models, and docking studies were performed with the representative homology model (see Experimental Section for details). The docking poses were carefully inspected and suggested two possible binding modes (poses A and B) for each compound. These poses differed in the binding of the isoquinoline core (Figure 8). Three distinct trials of 30 ns molecular dynamics (MD) simulations were conducted for each docking pose to assess the binding mode.(31) MD trajectories of the last 20 ns were used to calculate the root-mean-square deviation (RMSD) of the ligand heavy atoms, which were measured from the averaged structure of the merged MD simulations (20 ns ×3 trials), as shown in Figure 9. The RMSD profiles of compound 10 showed different behavior between the two binding modes, and the RMSD value of pose A was lower than that of pose B. Furthermore, similar RMSD profiles were obtained for pose A, whereas pose B exhibited different profiles between the three simulation trials, suggesting that pose A is more stable than pose B. In contrast, the RMSD profiles for poses A and B of compound 26 were similar, but pose B exhibited slightly lower RMSD values than pose A, suggesting that pose B may be more stable. Interestingly, SAR studies of 4,6-isoquinoline showed that the bicyclic aryl groups on the 4-position had relatively high activity, whereas the bicyclic aryl groups on the 7-position of 5,7-isoquinoline were not acceptable. These results indicated that the 4-aniline of 4,6-isoquinoline and the 7-aniline of 5,7-isoquinoline are placed on different positions, and the 4-aniline of 4,6-isoquinoline in particular was more extended and flexible than the 7-aniline of 5,7-isoquinoline. We concluded that pose A is the most probable binding pose for both compounds 10 and 26 based on the in silico and SAR results. The representative binding modes of compounds 10 (yellow) and 26 (magenta) are shown in Figure 10 and indicate that the isoquinoline core of both compounds binds very similarly.
    Next, we performed a fragment molecular orbital (FMO) analysis(32−35) to better understand the nature of the protein–ligand interactions. This is a quantum mechanical computational approach in which interfragment interaction energy (IFIE) analysis(34) allows examination of protein–ligand interactions with quantum effects at reasonable computational cost. Thus, in pair interaction energy decomposition analysis (PIEDA),(35) the interaction energy can be deconstructed into four energy terms: electrostatic, exchange repulsion, dispersion, and charge transfer with higher-order mixed terms. These four terms provide detailed information regarding the protein–ligand interactions. For example, the FMO study suggested the formation of hydrogen bonds between the nitrogen at position 2 of the isoquinoline core and the backbone −NH of the hinge residue V333 in both compounds 10 and 26, as expected (Supporting Information, Figure S3). Furthermore, dispersion interactions (∼ −3.8 kcal/mol), namely CH/π interaction, were observed between the isoquinoline core and L383. We also observed an unexpected dispersion interaction (−6.2 ∼ −4.7 kcal/mol) between the isoquinoline core and Y332, which might be a horizontally displaced π–π interaction from a geometric perspective. These observations suggest that the isoquinoline core is a favorable hinge binder and can interact with the hinge via electrostatic and dispersion interactions. The amine parts of the pyrrolidine and piperidine of compounds 10 and 26 are situated in similar positions and form strong electrostatic interactions with D380. The FMO calculations were performed under in vacuo conditions. In aqueous solvent, the desolvation energy upon protein–ligand binding would likely compensate to some extent for the electrostatic energies, and thus the electrostatic interaction energy shown in Supporting Information, Figure S3, does not fully contribute to the actual binding free energy.
    The 4-aniline moiety of compound 10 extended into the solvent region and showed electrostatic interaction with D337 mediated by a bridging water molecule (not present in Figure 10). MD simulation analysis also suggested that the 4-aniline moiety can locate close to D337 and directly form a hydrogen bond. Both cases are reasonable because inhibitor activity decreased 10-fold when the 4-aniline moiety was replaced with a benzene group, due to the lack of interaction with D337 (data not shown). In contrast, the 4-aniline moiety of compound 26 is extended into the c-helix region, thereby gaining additional interactions with F304, Y393, and Y395. These interactions are absent in compound 10, which may explain why the enzyme inhibitory activity of compound 26 (0.5 nM) was better than that for compound 10 (33 nM) and compound 26 (0.5 nM) (Figure 10 and Supporting Information, Figure S3).

    Chemistry

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To
    The synthesis of 10–21 is summarized in Scheme 1. Starting from commercially available 4-bromo-6-fluoro isoquinoline 45, etherification of the 6-fluoro position with alcohol and NaH gave the desired product 46 in good yield. Suzuki coupling on 4-bromo isoquinoline with dicyclohexyl(2′,6′-dimethoxy-[1,1′-biphenyl]-2-yl)phosphine (SPHOS)(36) and boronate 47a/boronic acids 47b–h, followed by deprotection of the Boc group with (trifluoroacetic acid) TFA, gave 4,6-disubstituted isoquinolines 10–14 and 19–21. Stille coupling with 48a and 48b and deprotection of the Boc group gave 15 and 16. Replacing 5-azaindole boronic ester with C–H insertion with palladium catalyst(37) and deprotection afforded 17 and 18.
    A summary of the synthesis of 22–25 is shown in Scheme 2. The etherification of 45 with 50a–d gave 51a–d, and subsequent Suzuki coupling and deprotection provided 22–25 in good yield.
    The synthesis of 26–32 is summarized in Scheme 3. Starting from commercially available 5-amino-7-chloroisoquinoline 52, reductive amination afforded 3-amino piperidine. Subsequent Suzuki coupling(36) and deprotection of the Boc group gave 53 in moderate yield. Using the same protocol, other aldehydes and ketones, and the amino-ethyl group or azepane structure, were introduced in place of 3-carbonyl-piperidine to provide 26–32.
    The synthesis of 33–36 (Scheme 4) involved an initial Suzuki coupling with 54 to afford 55 as the common intermediate, followed by reductive amination with tert-butyl (2-oxoethyl)-carbamate-carboxylate, then TFA, to afford 35 in good yield. Compounds 10, 11, and 13 that were synthesized using Sandmeyer conditions with 55 gave iodide derivative 56 in moderate yield. N-Arylation of 56 with Buchwald ligand 2-dicyclohexylphosphino-2′,4′,6′-triisopropylbiphenyl (XPHOS)(38) or 2-dicyclohexylphosphino-2′-(N,N-dimethylamino)biphenyl (DavePHOS),(39) followed by TFA, provided 33, 34, and 36.
    The derivatization of azepane 37–44 by N-arylation with 5-bromo-7-hydroxy-isoquinoline, followed by triflatation, provided the common intermediate 59. Subsequent Suzuki coupling with the corresponding boronic acid/pinacol ester afforded 37–44 in good yield (Scheme 5).

    Conclusions

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To
    In conclusion, we identified two potent and selective chemical series of PKCζ inhibitors, 21 and 37, using our systematical FBDD strategy. This strategy uses a unique isoquinoline fragment for biochemical screening against a high concentration of compounds, followed by merging the two fragments on the isoquinoline ring. This methodology can be applied to the discovery of other challenging kinase inhibitors. Our SAR-guided optimization effort rapidly identified potent 5,7-isoquinoline inhibitor 37. We also demonstrated the good in vivo efficacy of 37 in a CIA model. Therefore, PKCζ inhibition is a novel therapeutic target of RA and compound 37 is an advanced chemical tool with which to further explore the efficacy, safety, and tolerability of PKCζ ligands.

    Experimental Section

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To

    General Chemistry Information

    NMR spectra were run at 300 MHz on JEOL Co or 400 MHz on Brukar Co. As a solvent, deuterated chloroform was used, unless specifically described otherwise. For measurement of chemical shift, tetramethylsilane (TMS) was employed as an internal standard. Chemical shifts (δ) are expressed in ppm. Data for proton spectra are reported as follows: chemical shift (multiplicity [singlet (s), doublet (d), triplet (t), and multiplet (m)], coupling constants [Hz], integration). Carbon spectra were recorded with complete proton decoupling and the chemical shifts are reported in ppm. As a mass spectrometer, quadrupole type mass spectrometer, UPLC/SQD system manufactured by Waters Company was used and ionization was carried out based on an electrospray method (ESI) for the measurement. The liquid chromatography instrument used was Acquity Ultra Performance LC system. As a separation column, Acquity UPLC BEH (C18 2.1 mm × 50 mm 1.7 μM, Waters Company) was used. With respect to the examples and the reference examples in which specific descriptions are given for LC condition, the measurements were carried out by using any one of the above-described apparatuses and in accordance with the following solvent condition. In addition, m/z indicates mass spectrum data (M + H). Compound purity was determined using HPLC and is ≥95% for all test compounds.

    Preparation of Compounds 10–44, 46, 53, 55, 56, and 59

    General Procedure A: SNAr Reaction with NaH

    NaH (2.48 g, 65.61 mmol, 9.0 equiv) was added portionwise to a stirred solution of (S)-1-(tert-butoxycarbonyl)-3-pyrrolidinol (3.36 g, 17.9 mmol, 1.5 equiv) in DMA (40 mL) at 0 °C under air. After stirring for 30 min at 0 °C, 4-bromo-6-fluoro-isoquinoline 45 was added and stirred at 80 °C for 1.5 h. The reaction mixture was quenched with added H2O (50 mL), and the mixture was extracted with CHCl3 (3 × 50 mL). The combined organic extracts were dried (MgSO4) and evaporated under reduced pressure to give the crude product.

    General Procedure B: Suzuki–Miyaura Coupling with 47a–n

    Pd(OAc)2 (4.8 mg, 0.021 mmol, 0.2 equiv) was added dropwise to a stirred solution of 46, 51a–d, or 53 (40 mg, 0.105 mmol, 1.0 equiv), tert-butyl N-[4-(4,4,5,5-tetrametyl-1,3,2-dioxaborolane-2-yl)phenyl]carbamate 47a (67 mg, 0.21 mmol, 2.0 equiv), SPHOS (16.4 mg, 0.044 mmol, 0.4 equiv), and K3PO4 (46.8 mg, 0.21 mg, 2.0 equiv) in n-BuOH (4 mL) at rt. The resulting solution was stirred and heated at 110 °C for 18 h. After filtering out the precipitation, the mixture was evaporated under reduced pressure to give the crude product.

    General Procedure C: Deprotection of Boc Group

    HCl(aq) (35%, 500 μL, 5 mmol, 56 equiv) was added to a stirred solution of carbamate (44.6 mg, 0.088 mmol, 1.0 equiv) in MeOH (1.0 mL) at rt. The resulting solution was stirred at rt for 4 h. The solvent was removed in vacuo. Then, NH4OH(aq) (5 mL) was added and the mixture was extracted with CH2Cl2 (3 × 10 mL). The combined organic layers were dried (MgSO4) and evaporated under reduced pressure to give the crude product.

    General Procedure D: Stille Coupling with 48a and 48b

    Pd(PPh3)4 (30 mg, 0.026 mmol, 0.2 equiv) was added dropwise to a stirred solution of 46 (50 mg, 0.127 mmol, 1.0 equiv), 2-tributylstannylthiazole 48a (80 μL, 0.254 mmol, 2.0 equiv), and LiCl (11 mg, 0.254 mmol, 2.0 equiv) in toluene (1.3 mL) at rt. The resulting solution was stirred and heated at 110 °C for 18 h. After filtering out the precipitation, the mixture was evaporated under reduced pressure to give the crude product.

    General Procedure E: C–H Insertion Reaction with 49a and 49b

    Pd(OAc)2 (5.7 mg, 0.025 mmol, 0.2 equiv) was added dropwise to a stirred solution of 46 (50 mg, 0.127 mmol, 1.0 equiv), benzoxazole 49a (23 mg, 0.191 mmol, 1.5 equiv), CuBr (15 mg, 0.102 mmol, 0.8 equiv), Cs2CO3 (42 mg, 0.127 mmol, 1.0 equiv), and tBu3P (10 mg, 0.05 mmol, 0.4 equiv) in DMF (1 mL) at rt. The resulting solution was stirred and heated at 150 °C for 18 h. After filtering out the precipitation, the mixture was evaporated under reduced pressure to give the crude product.

    General Procedure F: Reductive Amination with 52 or 55

    tert-butyl 3-oxopiperidine-1-carboxylate or tert-butyl (2-oxoethyl)-carbamate-carboxylate (0.298 mmol, 2 equiv) was added dropwise to a stirred solution of amine 52 or 55 (50 mg, 0.149 mmol, 1.0 equiv) in AcOH (1.5 mL) at rt and stirred at rt for 30 min. Then NaBH(OAc)3 was added to a stirred reaction mixture and stirred at RT for 18 h. The mixture was evaporated under reduced pressure to give the crude product.

    General Procedure G: N-Arylation with Buchwald’s Catalyst with 56 or 58

    Pd2dba3 (110 mg, 0.12 mmol, 0.1 equiv) was added to a stirred solution of iodide 56 or bromide 58 (1.17 mmol, 1.0 equiv), amine 58a (250 mg, 1.174 mmol, 1.0 equiv), Davephos or XPHOS (0.468 mmol, 0.4 equiv), and tBuONa (225 mg, 2.34 mmol, 2.0 equiv) in 1,4-dioxane (12 mL) at rt. The resulting solution was stirred and heated at 110 °C for 18 h. After filtering out the precipitation, the mixture was evaporated under reduced pressure to give the crude product.

    General Procedure H: Suzuki–Miyaura Coupling with 47m or 47o–u

    PdCl2(dppf)2 (13.8 mg, 0.017 mmol, 0.2 equiv) was added dropwise to a stirred solution of triflate 59 (39.1 mg, 0.083 mmol, 1.0 equiv), boronic acid/boronate 47m or 47o–u (0.16 mmol, 2.0 equiv), and 2 M Na2CO3(aq) (320 μL, 0.16 mmol, 2.0 equiv) in THF (1 mL) at rt. The resulting solution was refluxed for 18 h. After filtering out the precipitation, the mixture was extracted with EtOAc, washed with Brine, dried over MgSO4, and evaporated under reduced pressure to give the crude product.

    (S)-4-(6-(Pyrrolidin-3-yloxy)isoquinolin-4-yl)aniline (10)

Purification by Yamazen automated chromatography with 30:70 hexane–EtOAc as eluent gave the 55 (1.59 g, 56%) as a brown solid. 1H NMR (DMSO-d6, 400 MHz) δ 10.07 (s, 1H), 9.16 (s, 1H), 8.35 (d, J = 5.9 Hz, 1H), 7.95 (d, J = 6.1 Hz, 1H), 7.75–7.64 (m, 4H), 7.50 (s, 1H), 7.19 (d, J = 1.7 Hz, 1H), 6.07 (s, 2H), 2.11–2.05 (m, 3H). 13C NMR (DMSO-d6, 101 MHz) δ 168.4, 152.5, 144.5, 140.8, 139.5, 139.0, 134.9, 129.8, 127.0, 124.1, 119.3, 115.4, 111.6, 109.2, 24.1. MS (ESI) m/z 278 [(M + H)+], RT 0.92 min. HRMS (ESI) calcd for C20H22N3O2 [M + H]+ 336.1712, found 336.1700.

    tert-Butyl (4-(5-Iodoisoquinolin-7-yl)phenyl)carbamate (56)

    tert-Butyl nitrite (860 μL, 7.16 mmol, 1.5 equiv) was added to a stirred solution of 55 (1.6 g, 4.77 mmol, 1.0 equiv) and I2 (1.82 g, 7.16 mmol, 1.5 equiv) in MeCN (60 mL) at rt and stirred at 80 °C for 1 h. The reaction mixture was quenched with saturated Na2S2O3(aq) (50 mL), extracted with EtOAc (100 mL × 3), washed with brine (100 mL), dried over MgSO4, and evaporated to give the crude product. MS (ESI) m/z 447 [(M + H)+], RT 2.07 min. HRMS (ESI) calcd for C20H19IN2O2 [M + H]+ 447.0569, found 447.0554. HRMS (ESI) calcd for C20H20IN2O2 [M + H]+ 447.0569, found 447.0559.

    tert-Butyl (S)-3-((7-(((Trifluoromethyl)sulfonyl)oxy)isoquinolin-5-yl)amino)azepane-1-carboxylate (59)

    Using general procedure G, (S)-1-Boc-3-aminohomo-piperidine (5.34 g, 24.9 mmol, 1.2 equiv), Pd2dba3 (960 mg, 2.08 mmol, 0.2 equiv), DavePhos (11 mg, 0.027 mmol, 0.4 equiv), tBuONa (4.0 g, 20.8 mmol, 2.0 equiv), and 58 (4.65 g, 20.8 mmol, 1.0 equiv) in 1,4-dioxane (200 mL) gave the crude product.     Measurement of Kinase Inhibitory Activity against PKCζ

    Materials

    Purified recombinant human PKCζ protein was purchased from Invitrogen (P2273). The PKCζ peptide substrate (biotin-KKKKRFSFKKSFKC) was synthesized by SIGMA GENOSYS. The detection antibody was generated in-house using PKCζ peptide as an antigen. Streptavidin-XL665 was purchased from CisBio (610SAXLB). All other materials were purchased from commercial sources (e.g., Sigma-Aldrich and Wako).

    Methods

    PKCζ full-length activity was measured by phosphorylation of the PKCζ peptide substrate with 10 μM ATP using the TR-FRET method. The enzyme reaction was conducted in 50 mM Tris-HCl (pH 7.5), 1 mM DTT, 5 mM MgCl2, 0.1% BSA, and 0.01% Triton X-100.
    To determine inhibitor Ki values, test compounds were incubated for 30 min at room temperature in the presence of PKCζ enzyme, 0.5 μM peptide substrate, and 10 μM ATP. The reaction was stopped with EDTA (66.7 mM), detection mix was added (europium-labeled antibody (0.3 μg/mL), streptavidin-XL665 (3.3 μg/mL), 50 mM Tris-HCl (pH 7.5), 0.1% BSA, and 200 mM KF), the mixture was incubated for 60 min at room temperature, then fluorescence was measured at 615 and 665 nm.

    Evaluation of Inhibitors on TNFα Production in THP-1 Cells Stimulated with LPS

    Cell Culture

    THP-1 monocytic cell line was cultured in Roswell Park Memorial Institute (RPMI) 1640 medium containing 10% fetal bovine serum (FBS) at 37 °C. Before treatments, cells were seeded at 1 × 105 cells/well on 96-well plates.

    Treatments of THP-1 Cells

    Test compounds (20 μL, 0.001, 0.01, 0.1, 1, 10, or 100 μM) containing 10% DMSO and 10% FBS/RPMI1640 were added to each well and preincubated for 60 min, then 20 μL of LPS (2–20 μg/mL of LPS in RPMI1640/10% FBS) was added, and the mixture was incubated for 4 h. After centrifugation for 2 min, 100 μL of the supernatant was removed and mixed with 5 μL of cell counting kit-8 solution and added to the remaining cell wells. Homogeneous time-resolved fluorescence (HTRF) from hTNFα was measured for each well at 450 nm.

    PK Study

    The PK activities of compounds 21 and 37 were studied using male C57BL/6N mice (Japan SLC, Inc.). Test compounds were administered intravenously at a dose of 1 mg/kg in saline or orally at a dose of 2 mg/kg in saline or water (dose volume 5 mL/kg) to mice (n = 2). Blood samples were collected via the subclavian vein at 0.5, 1, 2, 4, 6, and 8 h post dose. Plasma was separated by centrifugation and stored frozen at −20 °C until analysis. Plasma compound concentrations were determined using LC-MS/MS. PK parameters were calculated using the noncompartmental analysis tool provided with WinNonlin Enterprise software v6.4.0.768 (Certara).

    Mouse CIA (Collagen-Induced Arthritis) Model

    Preparation of Mouse CIA Model

    DBA/1J female mice, 7 weeks old, were purchased from Japan SLC, Inc. and housed five per cage. After 1 week of acclimatization, the mice were injected into the base of the tail with 100 μL of 2 mg/mL bovine type II collagen (C2; MCK, K-41, Japan) emulsified 6:5 with 1 mg/mL Freund’s complete adjuvant (CFA; DIFCO). This C2/CFA emulsion was also injected into mice as a booster immunization 21 days after the first immunization.
    The development of arthritis was evaluated in a blinded manner. Disease severity was scored three times a week. The severity of arthritis in each paw was scored from 0 to 3.
    No evidence of erythema and swelling received a score of 0, erythema and slight swelling confined to the toe, instep, or ankle joints received a score of 1, erythema and moderate swelling of the instep and ankle joints received a score of 2, and erythema and severe swelling extending from the toe to ankle joints received a score of 3. A total arthritis score was determined by summing the scores for all four paws.

    Evaluation of PKCζ Inhibitors

    We evaluated the anti-inflammatory effect of PKCζ inhibitors in mice by orally administering (p.o.) 37 (3, 10, 30 mg/kg) twice a day after booster immunization. The known PKCζ inhibitor BMS 345541 (60 mg/kg) was used as a positive control of PKCζ inhibition and was administrated (po) once a day. All inhibitors were dissolved in 0.5% aqueous methyl cellulose. The experimental protocols were approved by the experimental animal ethics committee at Asahi Kasei Pharma Corporation and were conducted in accordance with the guidelines concerning the management and handling of experimental animals.

    Modeling Studies

    Homology Modeling and Docking Simulation

    The crystal structure of PKCι (PDB 4DC2)(30) was used as a template to build the homology models. The identity of the amino acid sequence with respect to PKCζ is 72% overall, rising to 84% in the catalytic domain. The kinase domain (residues 252–518) of PKCι was used to build 100 homology models (10 main chain models × 10 side chain models) of PKCζ. The lowest energy conformer was selected as the representative homology model of PKCζ and was subsequently subjected to docking studies with compounds 10 and 26. The docking poses were carefully inspected and provided two possible binding modes for each compound (see text for details). Homology modeling and docking simulations were performed with MOE 2018.0101.

    MD Simulation

    The docking model candidates of compounds 10 and 26 were subjected to MD simulations to assess their predicted binding modes. In this study, MD simulations were performed using the GPU-accelerated version of AMBER14(40) using the Amber ff14SB force field(41) for proteins. The protonation states of the amino acid residues were treated in their general tautomer states under physiological conditions. Ligand force fields were parametrized using the general Amber force field (GAFF)(42) with AM1-BCC(43,44) charges. Rectangular simulation boxes were constructed with a margin of at least 10 Å between the protein and the periodic box boundaries solvated with TIP3P(45) water models. Electrostatic interactions were treated with the particle mesh Ewald method(46) in which Lennard-Jones interactions and real-space electrostatic interactions were smoothly switched at 9 Å. Each system was first minimized with harmonic positional restraints imposed on the protein backbone atoms with a force constant of 10 kcal mol–1 Å–2. Subsequently, the system was subjected to a 100 ps MD simulation with the NVT ensemble at 300 K, and 200 ps of the NPT ensemble at 300 K and 1 bar, where the restraints were gradually reduced to zero. Finally, three distinct 30 ns simulations were performed, where the first 10 ns were used to further relax the system and the last 20 ns were used for the RMSD analysis described in the main text. Isothermal and isobaric conditions were realized using the Langevin thermostat,(47,48) with a friction constant of 2.0 ps–1 and the Berendsen barostat(49) with a pressure relaxation time of 1.0 ps. The MD time step was 2 fs, with hydrogen bonds constrained using the SHAKE(50) and SETTLE(51) algorithms.

    FMO Calculation

    Representative binding modes of compounds 10 and 26 were manually selected from the MD trajectories and subjected to FMO calculations to better understand the protein–ligand interactions. The protein–ligand system and the 20 nearest water molecules to the ligand were extracted from the MD trajectory. The complex system was minimized prior to FMO calculations using the default setting of the PrepWizard module implemented in Schrodinger 2018-4, ensuring that the protonation states were not changed. Then, the minimized structures, including the 20 nearest water molecules, were subjected to FMO calculations with FMO2-MP2/6-31G using the FMO code version 18 embedded in the General Atomic and Molecular Electronic Structure System (GAMESS).(52)

      Newton, A. C. Protein kinase C: poised to signal. Am. J. Physiol. Endocrinol. Metab. 2010, 298, E395– 402,  DOI: 10.1152/ajpendo.00477.2009

      Protein kinase C: poised to signal
      Newton Alexandra C
      American journal of physiology. Endocrinology and metabolism (2010), 298 (3), E395-402 ISSN:.
      Nestled at the tip of a branch of the kinome, protein kinase C (PKC) family members are poised to transduce signals emanating from the cell surface. Cell membranes provide the platform for PKC function, supporting the maturation of PKC through phosphorylation, its allosteric activation by binding specific lipids, and, ultimately, promoting the downregulation of the enzyme. These regulatory mechanisms precisely control the level of signaling-competent PKC in the cell. Disruption of this regulation results in pathophysiological states, most notably cancer, where PKC levels are often grossly altered. This review introduces the PKC family and then focuses on recent advances in understanding the cellular regulation of its diacylglycerol-regulated members.

      PKCζ at the crossroad of NF-κB and Jak1/Stat6 signaling pathways
      Moscat, J.; Rennert, P.; Diaz-Meco, M. T.
      Cell Death and Differentiation (2006), 13 (5), 702-711CODEN: CDDIEK; ISSN:1350-9047. (Nature Publishing Group)
      A review. The atypical protein kinase C (PKC) isoforms (aPKC) have been implicated in the regulation of a no. of essential signaling events. Early studies using dominant-neg. mutants suggested that they are important intermediaries in the activation of the canonical nuclear factor (NF)-κB pathway. More recent data using knockout mice genetically demonstrate that in fact the PKCζ isoform is essential for the adequate activation of this cascade both upstream and downstream the IκB kinase complex. In this review, we summarize the mechanistic details whereby the aPKC pathway regulates important cellular functions and how this is achieved by the ability of these kinases to interact with different protein regulators and adapters, as well as to impinge in NF-κB-independent signaling cascades such as the Janus kinase-1/signal transducer and activator of transcription 6 system, which plays a crit. role in T-cell-mediated hepatitis and asthma.

      The atypical PKCs in inflammation: NF-κB and beyond
      Diaz-Meco, Maria T.; Moscat, Jorge
      Immunological Reviews (2012), 246 (1), 154-167, 14 pp.CODEN: IMRED2; ISSN:1600-065X. (Wiley-Blackwell)
      A review. From the very early days of nuclear factor-κB (NF-κB) research, it was recognized that different protein kinase C (PKC) isoforms might be involved in the activation of NF-κB. Pharmacol. tools and pseudosubstrate inhibitors suggested that these kinases play a role in this important inflammatory and survival pathway; however, it was the anal. of several genetic mouse knockout models that revealed the complexity and interrelations between the different components of the PB1 network in several cellular functions, including T-cell biol., bone homeostasis, inflammation assocd. with the metabolic syndrome, and cancer. These studies unveiled, for example, the crit. role of PKCζ as a pos. regulator of NF-κB through the regulation of RelA but also its inflammatory suppressor activities through the regulation of the interleukin-4 signaling cascade. This observation is of relevance in T cells, where p62, PKCζ, PKCλ/ι, and NBR1 establish a mesh of interactions that culminate in the regulation of T-cell effector responses through the modulation of T-cell polarity. Many questions remain to be answered, not just from the point of view of the implication for NF-κB activation but also with regard to the in vivo interplay between these pathways in pathophysiol. processes like obesity and cancer.

      Protein kinase Cξ (PKCξ): Activation mechanisms and cellular functions
      Hirai, Takaaki; Chida, Kazuhiro
      Journal of Biochemistry (2003), 133 (1), 1-7CODEN: JOBIAO; ISSN:0021-924X. (Japanese Biochemical Society)
      A review. Protein kinase C isoform ξ (PKC-ξ) is a member of the atypical PKC subfamily and has been widely implicated in the regulation of cellular functions. Increasing evidence from studies using in vitro and in vivo systems points to PKC-ξ as a key regulator of crit. intracellular signaling pathways induced by various extracellular stimuli. The major activation pathway of PKC-ξ depends on phosphatidylinositol (PI)-3,4,5-trisphosphate (PIP3), which is mainly produced by PI-3 kinase. 3'-PI-dependent protein kinase 1, which binds with high affinity to PIP3, phosphorylates and activates PKC-ξ. Many studies have demonstrated the involvement of PKC-ξ in the MAP kinase cascade, transcriptional factor NFκB activation, ribosomal protein S6 kinase signaling, and cell polarity. An important mol. event in a cell is the assocn. of PKC-ξ with other signaling mols., as well as scaffold proteins, to form large complexes that regulate their pathways. The understanding of the mechanisms underlying PKC-ξ-mediated control of intracellular signaling is beginning to provide important insights into the roles of PKC-ξ in various cells.

      PKCζ as a promising therapeutic target for TNFα-induced inflammatory disorders in chronic cutaneous wounds
      Zhang, Jian; Yang, Xuekang; Wang, Hongtao; Zhao, Bin; Wu, Xue; Su, Linlin; Xie, Songtao; Wang, Yunchuan; Li, Jun; Liu, Jiaqi; Liu, Mengdong; Han, Fu; He, Ting; Zhang, Wei; Tao, Ke; Hu, Dahai
      International Journal of Molecular Medicine (2017), 40 (5), 1335-1346CODEN: IJMMFG; ISSN:1791-244X. (Spandidos Publications Ltd.)
      Protein kinase Cζ (PKCζ) is a member of the atypical protein kinase C family. Its roles in macrophages or skin-resident keratinocytes have not been fully evaluated. In this study, we provide evidence that PKCζ mediates lipopolysaccharide (LPS)-induced tumor necrosis factor α (TNFα) gene expression in the mouse macrophage cell line, RAW264.7. TNFα has been proven to be one of the main culprits of chronic wounds and impaired acute wounds, which are characterized by excessive inflammation, enhanced proteolysis and reduced matrix deposition. Among the multiple effects of TNFα on keratinocytes, the induction of chemokines which are indispensable factors involved in the massive infiltration of various inflammatory cells into skin lesions serves as a crucial mechanism. In the present study, we found that PKCζ inhibitor or its specific siRNA inhibited the TNFα-induced upregulation in the levels of the chemokines, interleukin (IL)-8, monocyte chemotactic protein-1 (MCP-1) and intercellular cell adhesion mol.-1 (ICAM-1) in HaCaT keratinocytes. Moreover, under a disrupted inflammatory environment, activated keratinocytes can synthesize large amts. of matrix metalloproteinases (MMP), which has a neg. effect on tissue remodeling. We discovered that TNFα promoted the expression of MMP9 in a PKCζ-dependent manner. Further expts. revealed that nuclear factor-κB (NF-κB) was a key downstream mol. of PKCζ. In addn., as shown in vitro, PKCζ was not involved in the TNFα-induced decrease in HaCaT cell migration and proliferation. In vivo expts. demonstrated that TNFα-induced wound closure impairment and inflammatory disorders were significantly attenuated in the PKCζ inhibitor group. On the whole, our findings suggest that PKCζ is a crucial regulator in LPS- or TNFα-induced inflammatory responses in RAW264.7 cells and HaCaT keratinocytes, and that PKCζ/ NF-κB signaling may be a potential target for interventional therapy for TNFα-induced skin inflammatory injury.

      Regulation of NF-κB RelA phosphorylation and transcriptional activity by p21ras and protein kinase Cζ in primary endothelial cells
      Anrather, Josef; Csizmadia, Vilmos; Soares, Miguel P.; Winkler, Hans
      Journal of Biological Chemistry (1999), 274 (19), 13594-13603CODEN: JBCHA3; ISSN:0021-9258. (American Society for Biochemistry and Molecular Biology)
      The activity of the transcription factor NF-κB is thought to be regulated mainly through cytoplasmic retention by IκB mols. Here we present evidence of a second mechanism of regulation acting on NF-κB after release from IκB. In endothelial cells this mechanism involves phosphorylation of the RelA subunit of NF-κB through a pathway involving activation of protein kinase Cζ (PKCζ) and p21ras.       LaVallie, Edward R.; Chockalingam, Priya S.; Collins-Racie, Lisa A.; Freeman, Bethany A.; Keohan, Cristin C.; Leitges, Michael; Dorner, Andrew J.; Morris, Elisabeth A.; Majumdar, Manas K.; Arai, Maya

      Protein kinase Cζ (PKCζ) is an intracellular serine/threonine protein kinase that has been implicated in the signaling pathways for certain inflammatory cytokines, including interleukin-1 (IL-1) and tumor necrosis factor α (TNF-α), in some cell types.       Essential role of RelA Ser311 phosphorylation by ζPKC in NF-κB transcriptional activation
      Duran, Angeles; Diaz-Meco, Maria T.; Moscat, Jorge
      EMBO Journal (2003), 22 (15), 3910-3918CODEN: EMJODG; ISSN:0261-4189. (Oxford University Press)
      The activation of the transcription factor NF-κB is central to the control of the cellular response triggered by many stimuli. Once released from the inhibitory mol. IκB, NF-κB is translocated to the nucleus, and it has to be phosphorylated to activate transcription. In ζ protein kinase C (PKC)-deficient cells, NF-κB is transcriptionally inactive and the phosphorylation of the RelA subunit in response to tumor necrosis factor (TNF-α) is severely impaired. In vitro assays showed that ζPKC directly phosphorylates RelA. Here we demonstrate that Ser311 accounts for ζPKC phosphorylation of RelA and that this site is phosphorylated in vivo in response to TNF-α. Also, an inactivating mutation of that residue severely impairs RelA transcriptional activity, blocks its anti-apoptotic function and abrogates the interaction of RelA with the co-activator CBP as well as its recruitment, and that of RNA polymerase II (Pol II) with the interleukin-6 (IL-6) promoter. The interaction of endogenous CBP with endogenous RelA is inhibited in ζPKC-/- cells, as well as the binding of Pol II to the IL-6 promoter. These results demonstrate the mechanism whereby ζPKC regulates NF-κB activation in vivo.

      Protein Kinase C Isoforms ζ and ι Mediate Collagenase Expression and Cartilage Destruction via STAT3- and ERK-dependent c-fos Induction
      Litherland, Gary J.; Elias, Martina S.; Hui, Wang; MacDonald, Christopher D.; Catterall, Jonathon B.; Barter, Matt J.; Farren, Matthew J.; Jefferson, Matthew; Rowan, Andrew D.
      Journal of Biological Chemistry (2010), 285 (29), 22414-22425CODEN: JBCHA3; ISSN:0021-9258. (American Society for Biochemistry and Molecular Biology)
      The protein kinase C (PKC) signaling pathway is a major regulator of cellular functions and is implicated in pathologies involving extracellular matrix remodeling. Inflammatory joint disease is characterized by excessive extracellular matrix catabolism, and here we assess the role of PKC in the induction of the collagenases, matrix metalloproteinase (MMP)-1 and MMP-13, in human chondrocytes by the potent cytokine stimulus interleukin-1 (IL-1) in combination with oncostatin M (OSM). IL-1 + OSM-stimulated collagenolysis and gelatinase activity were ameliorated by pharmacol. PKC inhibition in bovine cartilage, as was collagenase gene induction in human chondrocytes. Small interfering RNA-mediated silencing of PKC gene expression showed that both novel (nPKCδ, nPKCη) and atypical (aPKCζ, aPKCι) isoforms were involved in collagenase induction by IL-1. However, MMP1 and MMP13 induction by IL-1 + OSM was inhibited only by aPKC silencing, suggesting that only atypical isoforms play a significant role in complex inflammatory milieus. Silencing of either aPKC led to diminished IL-1 + OSM-dependent extracellular signal-regulated kinase (ERK) and signal transducer and activator of transcription (STAT) 3 phosphorylation, and c-fos expression. STAT3 gene silencing or ERK pathway inhibition also resulted in loss of IL-1 + OSM-stimulated c-fos and collagenase expression. Silencing of c-fos and c-jun expression was sufficient to abrogate IL-1 + OSM-stimulated collagenase gene induction, and overexpression of both c-fos and c-jun was sufficient to drive transcription from the MMP1 promoter in the absence of a stimulus. Our data identify atypical PKC isoenzymes as STAT and ERK activators that mediate c-fos and collagenase expression during IL-1 + OSM synergy in human chondrocytes. APKCs may constitute potential therapeutic targets for inflammatory joint diseases involving increased collagenase expression.

In the present paper, we describe the identification and functional characterization of potent and selective thieno[2,3-d]pyrimidine-based chem. inhibitors of aPKCs. A crystal structure of human PKCι kinase domain bound to a representative compd., CRT0066854, reveals the basis for potent and selective chem. inhibition. Furthermore, CRT0066854 displaces a crucial Asn-Phe-Asp motif that is part of the adenosine-binding pocket and engages an acidic patch used by arginine-rich PKC substrates. We show that CRT0066854 inhibits the LLGL2 (lethal giant larvae 2) phosphorylation in cell lines and exhibits phenotypic effects in a range of cell-based assays. We conclude that this compd. can be used as a chem. tool to modulate aPKC activity in vitro and in vivo and may guide the search for further aPKC-selective inhibitors.

      4-benzimidazolyl-3-phenylbutanoic acids as novel PIF-pocket-targeting allosteric inhibitors of protein kinase PKCζ
      Frohner Wolfgang; Lopez-Garcia Laura A; Neimanis Sonja; Weber Nadja; Navratil Jeanette; Maurer Frauke; Stroba Adriana; Zhang Hua; Biondi Ricardo M; Engel Matthias
      Journal of medicinal chemistry (2011), 54 (19), 6714-23 ISSN:.
      Protein kinase inhibitors with an allosteric mode of action are expected to reach, in many cases, higher selectivity for the target enzyme than ATP-competitive compounds. Therefore, basic research is aiming at identifying and establishing novel sites on the catalytic domain of protein kinases which might be targeted by allosteric inhibitors. We previously published the first structure-activity relationships (SARs) for allosteric activators of protein kinase PDK1. Here, we present the design, synthesis, and SAR data on a series of novel compounds, 4-benzimidazolyl-3-phenylbutanoic acids, that inhibit the atypical protein kinace C (PKC) ζ via binding to the PIF-pocket. Key positions were identified in the compounds that can be modified to increase potency and selectivity. Some congeners showed a high selectivity toward PKCζ, lacking inhibition of the most closely related isoform, PKCι, and of further AGC kinases. Furthermore, evidence is provided that these compounds are also active toward cellular PKCζ without loss of potency compared to the cell-free assay.

      Discovery and Optimization of 1,3,5-Trisubstituted Pyrazolines as Potent and Highly Selective Allosteric Inhibitors of Protein Kinase C-ζ
      Abdel-Halim, Mohammad; Diesel, Britta; Kiemer, Alexandra K.; Abadi, Ashraf H.; Hartmann, Rolf W.; Engel, Matthias
      Journal of Medicinal Chemistry (2014), 57 (15), 6513-6530CODEN: JMCMAR; ISSN:0022-2623. (American Chemical Society)
      There is increasing evidence that the atypical protein kinase C, PKCζ, might be a therapeutic target in pulmonary and hepatic inflammatory diseases. However, targeting the highly conserved ATP-binding pocket in the catalytic domain held little promise to achieve selective inhibition. In the present study, the authors introduce 1,3,5-trisubstituted pyrazolines, e.g. I [R1 = 4-HO, 4-F, 4-Cl, etc.; R2 = Me, Ph, t-Bu, etc.; R3 = H, 4-Cl, 4-Br, etc.; R4 = H, Me] as potent and selective allosteric PKCζ inhibitors. The rigid scaffold offered many sites for modification, all acting as hot spots for improving activity, and gave rise to sharp structure-activity relationships. Targeting of PKCζ in cells was confirmed by reporter gene assay, transfection assays, and Western blotting. The strongly reduced cell-free and cellular activities toward a PIF-pocket mutant of PKCζ suggested that the inhibitors most likely bound to the PIF-pocket on the kinase catalytic domain. Thus, using a rigidification strategy and by establishing and optimizing multiple mol. interactions with the binding site, the authors were able to significantly improve the potency of the previously reported PKCζ inhibitors.

      Fragment-based lead discovery
      Rees, David C.; Congreve, Miles; Murray, Christopher W.; Carr, Robin
      Nature Reviews Drug Discovery (2004), 3 (8), 660-672CODEN: NRDDAG; ISSN:1474-1776. (Nature Publishing Group)
      A review. Fragment-based lead discovery is gaining momentum in both large pharmaceutical companies and biotechnol. labs. as a complementary approach to traditional screening. This is because fragment-based approaches require significantly fewer compds. to be screened and synthesized, and are showing a high success rate in generating chem. series with lead-like properties. Compared with traditional screening hits, the starting fragments have considerably lower mol. mass, and although the binding interactions of these fragments with a target protein are weak, they are structurally understood through x-ray crystallog. or NMR, and they exhibit high 'ligand efficiency'. Here, the authors use examples from 25 different protein targets to describe chem. strategies that exploit this structural knowledge to rapidly develop fragments into high-affinity leads.

      Lessons for fragment library design: analysis of output from multiple screening campaigns
      Chen, I.-Jen; Hubbard, Roderick E.
      Journal of Computer-Aided Molecular Design (2009), 23 (8), 603-620CODEN: JCADEQ; ISSN:0920-654X. (Springer)
      A review. Over the past 8 years, we have developed, refined and applied a fragment based discovery approach to a range of protein targets. Here we report computational analyses of various aspects of our fragment library and the results obtained for fragment screening. We reinforce the finding of others that the exptl. obsd. hit rate for screening fragments can be related to a computationally defined druggability index for the target. In general, the physicochem. properties of the fragment hits display the same profile as the library, as is expected for a truly diverse library which probes the relevant chem. space. An anal. of the fragment hits against various protein classes has shown that the physicochem. properties of the fragments are complementary to the properties of the target binding site. The effectiveness of some fragments appears to be achieved by an appropriate mix of pharmacophore features and enhanced aromaticity, with hydrophobic interactions playing an important role. The anal. emphasizes that it is possible to identify small fragments that are specific for different binding sites. To conclude, we discuss how the results could inform further development and improvement of our fragment library.

      Molecular Mechanism for the Regulation of Rho-Kinase by Dimerization and Its Inhibition by Fasudil
      Yamaguchi, Hiroto; Kasa, Miyuki; Amano, Mutsuki; Kaibuchi, Kozo; Hakoshima, Toshio
      Structure (Cambridge, MA, United States) (2006), 14 (3), 589-600CODEN: STRUE6; ISSN:0969-2126. (Cell Press)
      Rho-kinase is a key regulator of cytoskeletal events and a promising drug target in the treatment of vascular diseases and neurol. disorders. Unlike other protein kinases, Rho-kinase requires both N- and C-terminal extension segments outside the kinase domain for activity, although the details of this requirement have been elusive. The crystal structure of an active Rho-kinase fragment contg. the kinase domain and both the extensions revealed a head-to-head homodimer through the N-terminal extension forming a helix bundle that structurally integrates the C-terminal extension. This structural organization enables binding of the C-terminal hydrophobic motif to the N-terminal lobe, which defines the correct disposition of helix αC that is important for the catalytic activity. The bound inhibitor fasudil significantly alters the conformation and, consequently, the mode of interaction with the catalytic cleft that contains local structural changes. Thus, both kinase and drug conformational pliability and stability confer selectivity.

      Tsai, James; Lee, John T.; Wang, Weiru; Zhang, Jiazhong; Cho, Hanna; Mamo, Shumeye; Bremer, Ryan; Gillette, Sam; Kong, Jun; Haass, Nikolas K.; Sproesser, Katrin; Li, Ling; Smalley, Keiran S.       BRAFV600E is the most frequent oncogenic protein kinase mutation known. Furthermore, inhibitors targeting "active" protein kinases have demonstrated significant utility in the therapeutic repertoire against cancer. Therefore, the authors pursued the development of specific kinase inhibitors targeting B-Raf, and the V600E allele in particular. By using a structure-guided discovery approach, a potent and selective inhibitor of active B-Raf has been discovered. PLX4720, a 7-azaindole deriv. that inhibits B-RafV600E with an IC50 of 13 nM, defines a class of kinase inhibitor with marked selectivity in both biochem. and cellular assays. In B-RafV600E-dependent tumor xenograft models, orally dosed PLX4720 causes significant tumor growth delays, including tumor regressions, without evidence of toxicity. The work described here represents the entire discovery process, from initial identification through structural and biol. studies in animal models to a promising therapeutic for testing in cancer patients bearing B-RafV600E-driven tumors.

      Exploring the Stability of Ligand Binding Modes to Proteins by Molecular Dynamics Simulations: A Cross-docking Study
      Liu, Kai; Kokubo, Hironori
      Journal of Chemical Information and Modeling (2017), 57 (10), 2514-2522CODEN: JCISD8; ISSN:1549-9596. (American Chemical Society)
      Docking has become an indispensable approach in drug discovery research to predict the binding mode of a ligand. One great challenge in docking is to efficiently refine the correct pose from various putative docking poses through score functions. The authors recently examd. the stability of self-docking poses under mol. dynamics (MD) simulations and showed that equil. MD simulations have some capability to discriminate between correct and decoy poses. Here, the authors have extended the previous work to cross-docking studies for practical applications. Three target proteins (thrombin, heat shock protein 90-alpha, and cyclin-dependent kinase 2) of pharmaceutical interest were selected. Three comparable poses (one correct pose and two decoys) for each ligand were then selected from the docking poses. To obtain the docking poses for the three target proteins, the authors used three different protocols; namely, normal docking, induced fit docking (IFD), and IFD against the homol. model. Finally, five parallel MD equil. runs were performed on each pose for the statistical anal. The results showed that the correct poses were generally more stable than the decoy poses under MD. The discrimination capability of MD depends on the strategy. The safest way was to judge a pose as being stable if any one run among five parallel runs was stable under MD. In this case, 95% of the correct poses were retained under MD, and about 25-44% of the decoys could be excluded by the simulations for all cases. On the other hand, if the authors judge a pose as being stable when any two or three runs were stable, with the risk of incorrectly excluding some correct poses, approx. 31-53% or 39-56% of the two decoys could be excluded by MD, resp. The results suggest that simple equil. simulations can serve as an effective filter to exclude decoy poses that cannot be distinguished by docking scores from the computationally expensive free-energy calcns.

      Pair interaction molecular orbital method: an approximate computational method for molecular interactions
      Kitaura, Kazuo; Sawai, Takuya; Asada, Toshio; Nakano, Tatsuya; Uebayasi, Masami
      Chemical Physics Letters (1999), 312 (2-4), 319-324CODEN: CHPLBC; ISSN:0009-2614. (Elsevier Science B.V.)
      We propose an approx. method for calcg. large mol. clusters using MO methods. The method allows one to obtain the total energy of a mol. cluster by performing MO calcns. on the mols. and the mol. pairs in the system. The computational time is greatly reduced for a system contg. a large no. of mols., since the supermol. calcn. of the whole system is avoided. Our method considers important terms of many-body interactions within a pair approxn. and is expected to give results of reasonable accuracy even for mol. interactions having significant many-body effects. The numerical calcns. of water trimers and tetramers revealed that our method included the major portion of the many-body energies.

      Fragment molecular orbital method: an approximate computational method for large molecules
      Kitaura, K.; Ikeo, E.; Asada, T.; Nakano, T.; Uebayasi, M.

      An approx. MO method was proposed for calcg. large mols. such as proteins. Numerical calcns. were performed on C3H8, PrOH and AcNHMe to show the accuracy of the method.       Pair interaction energy decomposition analysis
      Fedorov, Dmitri G.; Kitaura, Kazuo
      Journal of Computational Chemistry (2007), 28 (1), 222-237CODEN: JCCHDD; ISSN:0192-8651. (John Wiley & Sons, Inc.)
      The energy decompn. anal. (EDA) by Kitaura and Morokuma was redeveloped in the framework of the fragment MO method (FMO). The proposed pair interaction energy decompn. anal. (PIEDA) can treat large mol. clusters and the systems in which fragments are connected by covalent bonds, such as proteins. The interaction energy in PIEDA is divided into the same contributions as in EDA: the electrostatic, exchange-repulsion, and charge transfer energies, to which the correlation (dispersion) term was added. The careful comparison to the ab initio EDA interaction energies for water clusters with 2-16 mols. revealed that PIEDA has the error of at most 1.2 kcal/mol (or about 1%). The anal. was applied to (H2O)1024, the α helix, β turn, and β strand of polyalanine (ALA)10, as well as to the synthetic protein (PDB code 1L2Y) with 20 residues. The comparative aspects of the polypeptide isomer stability are discussed in detail.

We present here a condensed vision of what Amber currently supports and where things are likely to head over the coming years. Figure 1 shows the performance in ns/day of the Amber package version 12 on a single-core AMD FX-8120 8-Core 3.6GHz CPU, the Cray XT5 system, and a single GPU GTX680.

      ff14SB: Improving the Accuracy of Protein Side Chain and Backbone Parameters from ff99SB
      Maier, James A.; Martinez, Carmenza; Kasavajhala, Koushik; Wickstrom, Lauren; Hauser, Kevin E.; Simmerling, Carlos
      Journal of Chemical Theory and Computation (2015), 11 (8), 3696-3713CODEN: JCTCCE; ISSN:1549-9618. (American Chemical Society)
      Mol. mechanics is powerful for its speed in atomistic simulations, but an accurate force field is required. The Amber ff99SB force field improved protein secondary structure balance and dynamics from earlier force fields like ff99, but weaknesses in side chain rotamer and backbone secondary structure preferences have been identified. Here, we performed a complete refit of all amino acid side chain dihedral parameters, which had been carried over from ff94. The training set of conformations included multidimensional dihedral scans designed to improve transferability of the parameters. Improvement in all amino acids was obtained as compared to ff99SB. Parameters were also generated for alternate protonation states of ionizable side chains. Av. errors in relative energies of pairs of conformations were under 1.0 kcal/mol as compared to QM, reduced 35% from ff99SB. We also took the opportunity to make empirical adjustments to the protein backbone dihedral parameters as compared to ff99SB. Multiple small adjustments of φ and ψ parameters were tested against NMR scalar coupling data and secondary structure content for short peptides. The best results were obtained from a phys. motivated adjustment to the φ rotational profile that compensates for lack of ff99SB QM training data in the β-ppII transition region. Together, these backbone and side chain modifications (hereafter called ff14SB) not only better reproduced their benchmarks, but also improved secondary structure content in small peptides and reprodn. of NMR χ1 scalar coupling measurements for proteins in soln. We also discuss the Amber ff12SB parameter set, a preliminary version of ff14SB that includes most of its improvements.

      Development and testing of a general Amber force field
      Wang, Junmei; Wolf, Romain M.; Caldwell, James W.; Kollman, Peter A.; Case, David A.
      Journal of Computational Chemistry (2004), 25 (9), 1157-1174CODEN: JCCHDD; ISSN:0192-8651. (John Wiley & Sons, Inc.)
      We describe here a general Amber force field (GAFF) for org. mols. GAFF is designed to be compatible with existing Amber force fields for proteins and nucleic acids, and has parameters for most org. and pharmaceutical mols. that are composed of H, C, N, O, S, P, and halogens. It uses a simple functional form and a limited no. of atom types, but incorporates both empirical and heuristic models to est. force consts. and partial at. charges. The performance of GAFF in test cases is encouraging. In test I, 74 crystallog. structures were compared to GAFF minimized structures, with a root-mean-square displacement of 0.26 Å, which is comparable to that of the Tripos 5.2 force field (0.25 Å) and better than those of MMFF 94 and CHARMm (0.47 and 0.44 Å, resp.). In test II, gas phase minimizations were performed on 22 nucleic acid base pairs, and the minimized structures and intermol. energies were compared to MP2/6-31G* results. The RMS of displacements and relative energies were 0.25 Å and 1.2 kcal/mol, resp. These data are comparable to results from Parm99/RESP (0.16 Å and 1.18 kcal/mol, resp.), which were parameterized to these base pairs. Test III looked at the relative energies of 71 conformational pairs that were used in development of the Parm99 force field. The RMS error in relative energies (compared to expt.) is about 0.5 kcal/mol. GAFF can be applied to wide range of mols. in an automatic fashion, making it suitable for rational drug design and database searching.

      Fast, efficient generation of high-quality atomic charges. AM1-BCC model: I. Method
      Jakalian, Araz; Bush, Bruce L.; Jack, David B.; Bayly, Christopher I.
      Journal of Computational Chemistry (2000), 21 (2), 132-146CODEN: JCCHDD; ISSN:0192-8651. (John Wiley & Sons, Inc.)
      The AM1-BCC method quickly and efficiently generates high-quality at. charges for use in condensed-phase simulations. The underlying features of the electron distribution including formal charge and delocalization are first captured by AM1 at. charges for the individual mol. Bond charge corrections (BCCs), which have been parameterized against the HF/6-31G* electrostatic potential (ESP) of a training set of compds. contg. relevant functional groups, are then added using a formalism identical to the consensus BCI (bond charge increment) approach. As a proof of the concept, we fit BCCs simultaneously to 45 compds. including O-, N-, and S-contg. functionalities, aroms., and heteroaroms., using only 41 BCC parameters. AM1-BCC yields charge sets of comparable quality to HF/6-31G* ESP-derived charges in a fraction of the time while reducing instabilities in the at. charges compared to direct ESP-fit methods. We then apply the BCC parameters to a small "test set" consisting of aspirin, D-glucose, and eryodictyol; the AM1-BCC model again provides at. charges of quality comparable with HF/6-31G* RESP charges, as judged by an increase of only 0.01 to 0.02 at. units in the root-mean-square (RMS) error in ESP. Based on these encouraging results, we intend to parameterize the AM1-BCC model to provide a consistent charge model for any org. or biol. mol.

      Fast, efficient generation of high-quality atomic charges. AM1-BCC model: II. parameterization and validation
      Jakalian, Araz; Jack, David B.; Bayly, Christopher I.
      Journal of Computational Chemistry (2002), 23 (16), 1623-1641CODEN: JCCHDD; ISSN:0192-8651. (John Wiley & Sons, Inc.)
      We present the first global parameterization and validation of a novel charge model, called AM1-BCC, which quickly and efficiently generates high-quality at. charges for computer simulations of org. mols. in polar media. The goal of the charge model is to produce at. charges that emulate the HF/6-31G* electrostatic potential (ESP) of a mol. Underlying electronic structure features, including formal charge and electron delocalization, are first captured by AM1 population charges; simple additive bond charge corrections (BCCs) are then applied to these AM1 at. charges to produce the AM1-BCC charges. The parameterization of BCCs was carried out by fitting to the HF/6-31G* ESP of a training set of >2700 mols. Most org. functional groups and their combinations were sampled, as well as an extensive variety of cyclic and fused bicyclic heteroaryl systems. The resulting BCC parameters allow the AM1-BCC charging scheme to handle virtually all types of org. compds. listed in The Merck Index and the NCI Database. Validation of the model was done through comparisons of hydrogen-bonded dimer energies and relative free energies of solvation using AM1-BCC charges in conjunction with the 1994 Cornell et al. forcefield for AMBER. Homo-dimer and hetero-dimer hydrogen-bond energies of a diverse set of org. mols. were reproduced to within 0.95 kcal/mol RMS deviation from the ab initio values, and for DNA dimers the energies were within 0.9 kcal/mol RMS deviation from ab initio values. The calcd. relative free energies of solvation for a diverse set of monofunctional isosteres were reproduced to within 0.69 kcal/mol of expt. In all these validation tests, AMBER with the AM1-BCC charge model maintained a correlation coeff. above 0.96. Thus, the parameters presented here for use with the AM1-BCC method present a fast, accurate, and robust alternative to HF/6-31G* ESP-fit charges for general use with the AMBER force field in computer simulations involving org. small mols.

      A smooth particle mesh Ewald method
      Essmann, Ulrich; Perera, Lalith; Berkowitz, Max L.; Darden, Tom; Lee, Hsing; Pedersen, Lee G.

      The previously developed particle mesh Ewald method is reformulated in terms of efficient B-spline interpolation of the structure factors. The authors demonstrate that arbitrary accuracy can be achieved, independent of system size N, at a cost that scales as N log(N). For biomol. systems with many thousands of atoms and this method permits the use of Ewald summation at a computational cost comparable to that of a simple truncation method of 10 Å or less.

      47
      Langevin dynamics of peptides: the frictional dependence of isomerization rates of N-acetylalanyl-N'-methylamide
      Loncharich, Richard J.; Brooks, Bernard R.; Pastor, Richard W.
      Biopolymers (1992), 32 (5), 523-35CODEN: BIPMAA; ISSN:0006-3525.
      The rate const. for the transition between the equatorial and axial conformations of N-acetylalanyl-N'-methylamide has been detd. from Langevin dynamics (LD) simulations with no explicit solvent. The isomerization rate is max. at collision frequency γ = 2 ps-1, shows diffusive character for γ ≥ 10 ps-1, but does not approach zero even at γ = 0.01 ps-1. This behavior differs from that found for a one-dimensional bistable potential and indicates that both collisional energy transfer with solvent and vibrational energy transfer between internal modes are important in the dynamics of barrier crossing for this system. It is suggested that conformational searches of peptides be carried out using LD with a collision frequency that maximizes the isomerization rate (i.e., γ ≈ 2 ps-1). This method is expected to be more efficient than either mol. dynamics in vacuo (which corresponds to LD with γ = 0) or mol. dynamics in solvent (where dynamics is largely diffusive).
